Yusof</a></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Template:Prostitution in the United Kingdom"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Template talk:Prostitution in the United Kingdom"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Phone_box_prostitute_calling_cards_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Phone_box_prostitute_calling_cards_1.jpg/220px-Phone_box_prostitute_calling_cards_1.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Phone_box_prostitute_calling_cards_1.jpg/330px-Phone_box_prostitute_calling_cards_1.jpg 1.5x, <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Phone_box_prostitute_calling_cards_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a href="/wiki/Tart_card" title="Tart card">Tart cards</a>" in <a href="/wiki/Telephone_booth" title="Telephone booth">phone boxes</a> advertise the services of <a href="/wiki/Call_girl" title="Call girl">call girls</a> in London (placing them in phone boxes is illegal, but they are very common.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>)</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Models,_11_Brewer_Street,_Soho_(2).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Models%2C_11_Brewer_Street%2C_Soho_%282%29.JPG/220px-Models%2C_11_Brewer_Street%2C_Soho_%282%29.JPG" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Models%2C_11_Brewer_Street%2C_Soho_%282%29.JPG/330px-Models%2C_11_Brewer_Street%2C_Soho_%282%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Models%2C_11_Brewer_Street%2C_Soho_%282%29.JPG/440px-Models%2C_11_Brewer_Street%2C_Soho_%282%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="4000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Models,_11_Brewer_Street,_Soho_(2).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A doorway advertising "Models" in <a href="/wiki/Brewer_Street" title="Brewer Street">Brewer Street</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soho" title="Soho">Soho</a>, London, an example of a <a href="/wiki/Soho_walk-up" title="Soho walk-up">Soho walk-up</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>In Great Britain (England, Wales and Scotland), prostitution itself (the exchange of sexual services for money) is legal,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> but a number of related activities, including <a href="/wiki/Soliciting" class="mw-redirect" title="Soliciting">soliciting</a> in a public place, <a href="/wiki/Kerb_crawler" title="Kerb crawler">kerb crawling</a>, owning or managing a <a href="/wiki/Brothel" title="Brothel">brothel</a>, pimping and <a href="/wiki/Procuring_(prostitution)" title="Procuring (prostitution)">pandering</a>, are crimes. In Northern Ireland, which previously had similar laws, paying for sex became illegal from 1 June 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In England and Wales, it is an offence to pay for sex with a prostitute who has been "subjected to force" and this is a <a href="/wiki/Strict_liability" title="Strict liability">strict liability</a> offence (clients can be prosecuted even if they did not know the prostitute was forced).\n</p><p>Laws are not always strictly enforced, and there are reports of police forces turning a blind eye to brothels.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian29March2016_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian29March2016-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Many brothels in cities such as Manchester and London operate under the name "<a href="/wiki/Massage_parlour" class="mw-redirect" title="Massage parlour">massage parlours</a>".\n</p><p>Throughout Great Britain it is illegal to buy sex from a person younger than 18, although the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_consent" title="Age of consent">age of consent</a> for non-commercial sex is 16 throughout the United Kingdom.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Extent"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Extent</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Medieval_period"><span century</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#20th_century"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">20th century</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#21st_century"><span class="tocnumber">2.5</span> <span class="toctext">21st century</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Current_legal_status"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Current legal status</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#England_and_Wales"><span class="toclevel-3 tocsection-12"><a href="#Prostitutes_cautions"><span class="tocnumber">3.1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Prostitutes cautions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-13"><a href="#Customers"><span class="tocnumber">3.1.4</span> <span class="toctext">Customers</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-14"><a href="#Third_parties"><span class="tocnumber">3.1.5</span> <span class="toctext">Third parties</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-15"><a href="#Advertising"><span class="tocnumber">3.1.6</span> <span class="toctext">Advertising</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Northern_Ireland"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Northern Ireland</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Scotland"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Scotland</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Reform_of_prostitution_laws"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Reform of prostitution laws</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Public_opinion"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Public opinion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Regulation"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Regulation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Decriminalisation"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Decriminalisation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#The_&quot;Nordic_model&quot;_of_prostitution"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">The "Nordic model" of prostitution</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Crimes_against_prostitutes"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Crimes against prostitutes</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Serial_murders"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Serial murders</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Sex_trafficking"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Sex trafficking</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Notable_figures_in_UK_prostitution"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Notable figures in UK prostitution</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#Historical_bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Historical bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extent">Extent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Extent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The total number of prostitutes in the United Kingdom is not known exactly and is difficult to assess. In 2009 authorities and NGOs estimated that approximately 100,000 persons in the country were engaged in prostitution.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Research published in 2015 indicated that there were approximately 72,800 sex workers in the UK; 88% were women, 6% men and 4% transgender.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> According to a 2009 study by <a href="/wiki/TAMPEP" title="TAMPEP">TAMPEP</a>, of all prostitutes in the UK, 41% were foreigners; however, in London this percentage was 80%. The total number of migrant prostitutes was significantly lower than in other Western countries (such as Spain and Italy where the percentage of all migrant prostitutes was 90%). The migrant prostitutes came from: Central Europe 43%, the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_states" title="Baltic states">Baltic states</a> 10%, Eastern Europe 7%, the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> 4%, other EU countries 16%, Latin America 10%, Asia, 7%, Africa 2%, North America 1%. Thirty-five different countries of origin were identified.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> According to data from the <a href="/wiki/Office_for_National_Statistics" title="Office for National Statistics">Office for National Statistics</a>, prostitution contributed \xc2\xa35.3&#160;billion to the UK economy in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In 2015 the <a href="/wiki/HM_Revenue_and_Customs" title="HM Revenue and Customs">HMRC</a> set up a dedicated "adult entertainment task force" to collect unpaid income tax from, among others, online <a href="/wiki/Escort_agency" title="Escort agency">escort agencies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The sex trade in the UK takes diverse forms, including street prostitution, escort prostitution and prostitution conducted from <a href="/wiki/Premises" title="Premises">premises</a>. The premises used include <a href="/wiki/Massage_parlour" class="mw-redirect" title="Massage parlour">massage parlours</a>, saunas, private flats and <a href="/wiki/Soho_walk-up" title="Soho walk-up">Soho walk-ups</a>. In 2003 undercover police visited the <a href="/wiki/Lap_dance" title="Lap dance">lap dancing</a> club <a href="/wiki/Spearmint_Rhino" title="Spearmint Rhino">Spearmint Rhino</a> on <a href="/wiki/Tottenham_Court_Road" title="Tottenham Court Road">Tottenham Court Road</a> in London and claimed that it was a front for prostitution.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In 2008 a study compiled by <a href="/wiki/The_Poppy_Project" class="mw-redirect" title="The Poppy Project">the Poppy Project</a> found brothels in all 33 London local authority areas. <a href="/wiki/Westminster" title="Westminster">Westminster</a> had the highest number with 71, compared with 8 in <a href="/wiki/Southwark" title="Southwark">Southwark</a>. For this study the researchers had posed as potential customers and had telephoned 921 brothels that had advertised in local newspapers. The researchers estimated that the brothels generated between \xc2\xa350m and \xc2\xa3130m a year. Many brothels operated through legitimate businesses which were licensed as saunas or massage parlours. However, the vast majority were in private flats in residential areas. The report found 77 different ethnicities among the prostitutes, with many from areas such as Eastern Europe and South-East Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The study has been called "the most comprehensive study ever conducted into UK brothels" but its methodology has been criticised, and it has been rejected by sex workers\' activists and academic studies.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The size of brothels in the UK is often small; Cari Mitchell, speaking for the <a href="/wiki/English_Collective_of_Prostitutes" title="English Collective of Prostitutes">English Collective of Prostitutes</a> in 2008, said that "most brothels are discreetly run by two or three women, sometimes with a receptionist, or one woman, usually an ex-sex worker who employs two or three others".<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> There were 55 prosecutions for brothel-keeping in 2013\xe2\x80\x9314 and 96 in 2014\xe2\x80\x9315.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In 2017 it was reported that some properties were being rented for a short time for use as "pop-up" brothels, sometimes in isolated areas.<sup id="cite_ref-pop_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pop-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Surveys indicate that fewer British men use prostitutes than in other countries. Estimates of between 7%<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> (1991 data) and 11%<sup id="cite_ref-The_prevalence_of,_and_factors_associated_with,_paying_for_sex_among_men_resident_in_Britain_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_prevalence_of,_and_factors_associated_with,_paying_for_sex_among_men_resident_in_Britain-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> (2010\xe2\x80\x932012 data) of men in the UK have used prostitutes at least once, compared to 15%\xe2\x80\x9320% in the USA or 16% in France. The authors stress the difficulty of finding reliable data given the lack of prior research, differences in sample sizes, and possible underestimates due to the privacy concerns of survey respondents.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2004 survey of street-based sex workers found that the average age of entry into prostitution was 21.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In March 2015 the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Leeds" title="University of Leeds">University of Leeds</a>, funded by the <a href="/wiki/Wellcome_Trust" title="Wellcome Trust">Wellcome Trust</a>, published one of the largest ever UK surveys of prostitutes. It found that 71% of prostitutes had previously worked in health, social care, education, childcare or charities, and that 38% held an undergraduate degree.<sup id="cite_ref-DianeTaylor_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DianeTaylor-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> A study published by <a href="/wiki/Swansea_University" title="Swansea University">Swansea University</a> in March 2015 found that nearly 5% of UK students had been involved in sex work in some capacity, including prostitution. Most students went into sex work to cover living expenses (two-thirds) and to pay off debts (45%).<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Approximately 70% of sex workers were indoor workers.<sup id="cite_ref-DianeTaylor_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DianeTaylor-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2016 the <a href="/wiki/Home_Affairs_Select_Committee" title="Home Affairs Select Committee">Home Affairs Select Committee</a> conducted its first ever enquiry into the sex industry.<sup id="cite_ref-HOSC_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOSC-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Evidence submitted to the enquiry indicated that Britain had approximately 70,000 prostitutes who earned an average of \xc2\xa32,000 a week. Submissions said that sex workers in Britain charged an average of \xc2\xa378 for services and had around 25 clients per week.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Around a quarter were said to be street prostitutes, the rest working from brothels and massage parlours. Reasons for choosing to work in prostitution included <a href="/wiki/Homelessness" title="Homelessness">homelessness</a> and addiction to drugs. In addition, an increasing number of <a href="/wiki/Single_parent" title="Single parent">single parents</a> were said to be opting to work as prostitutes to provide for their families.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The committee recommended that, given the current absence of robust data on the subject, the Home Office should commission a research study to inform future legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-HOSC_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOSC-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>One of the earliest pieces of evidence for prostitution in the country was given by the discovery on the banks of the River Thames of a Roman <a href="/wiki/Spintria" title="Spintria">spintria</a>, a small bronze token depicting a man and a woman engaged in a sexual act. Some scholars have suggested that spintria are brothel tokens, used to obtain entry to brothels or pay prostitutes.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_period">Medieval period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Medieval period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Many of London\'s Medieval brothels were located in the part of <a href="/wiki/Southwark" title="Southwark">Southwark</a> which fell under the <a href="/wiki/Jurisdiction" title="Jurisdiction">jurisdiction</a> of <a href="/wiki/Winchester_Palace" title="Winchester Palace">Winchester Palace</a>, the residence of the <a href="/wiki/Bishops_of_Winchester" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishops of Winchester">Bishops of Winchester</a>. In 1161 a parliament of <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II</a> introduced regulations allowing the Bishops to licence brothels and prostitutes in the area, which became known as the <a href="/wiki/Liberty_of_the_Clink" title="Liberty of the Clink">Liberty of the Clink</a>. As a result brothels multiplied in the <a href="/wiki/Bankside" title="Bankside">Bankside</a> part of the Liberty. They were popularly known as "stew-houses" as many were also steam-filled bath houses.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The bishop was their landlord, and they were often shut down when parliament was in session for the sake of appearance. Records of court proceedings indicate that priests, monks and friars were among their clients.<sup id="cite_ref-sin_city_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sin_city-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The brothels had to allow weekly searches by constables or bailiffs, and could not charge prostitutes more than 14 pence per week for a room. Opening was not permitted on holidays, and <a href="/wiki/Forced_prostitution" title="Forced prostitution">forced prostitution</a> was prohibited. Prostitutes were not allowed to live at the brothels or to be married, and they were required to spend a full night with their clients. These were the earliest laws in European to regulate prostitution, rather than suppressing it,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> and they provided a significant income for the Bishops. It is thought that the prostitutes, known as Winchester Geese, may have been buried in unconsecrated land at the <a href="/wiki/Cross_Bones" title="Cross Bones">Cross Bones</a> burial ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A series of regulations followed aimed at restricting London\'s prostitution to Southwark and narrowing its appeal.<sup id="cite_ref-sin_city_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sin_city-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/City_of_London" title="City of London">City of London</a> in 1277, prostitutes who worked in brothels were prohibited from living within the city walls.<sup id="cite_ref-Karras_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karras-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless there are indications that prostitution took place in the City in areas such as <a href="/wiki/Farringdon_Without" title="Farringdon Without">Farringdon Without</a>, a frequent haunt of "common women", and also in the neighbourhood between <a href="/wiki/Cheapside" title="Cheapside">Cheapside</a> and the church of <a href="/wiki/St_Pancras,_Soper_Lane" title="St Pancras, Soper Lane">St Pancras, Soper Lane</a>, a notorious district of sexual vice including one street called <a href="/wiki/Gropecunt_Lane" title="Gropecunt Lane">Gropecunt Lane</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sin_city_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sin_city-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In 1310 <a href="/wiki/Edward_II_of_England" title="Edward II of England">Edward II</a> ordered the abolition of London\'s brothels.<sup id="cite_ref-Karras_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karras-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most other towns and cities in Medieval England had brothels, and in some places the brothels were official and publicly-owned. Prostitutes were generally only allowed to ply their trade on specified streets or in designated areas. <a href="/wiki/Sumptuary_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Sumptuary laws">Sumptuary laws</a> were often passed requiring prostitutes to dress differently from other women who were considered "respectable".<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Laws varied form town to town, and prostitution in a particular locale was either regulated, allowed <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> if not <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a></i>, or outlawed. The regulation of prostitution in England lasted until 1546, when a fear that brothels were contributing to the spread of <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a> resulted in <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a> issuing a royal proclamation. This outlawed all of the brothels in England<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> and ended "toleration" for prostitutes, who were referred to as "dissolute and miserable persons".<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="17th_and_18th_centuries">17th and 18th centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 17th and 18th centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hogarth-Harlot-1_crop.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Hogarth-Harlot-1_crop.png/220px-Hogarth-Harlot-1_crop.png" width="220" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Hogarth-Harlot-1_crop.png/330px-Hogarth-Harlot-1_crop.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Hogarth-Harlot-1_crop.png/440px-Hogarth-Harlot-1_crop.png 2x" data-file-width="894" data-file-height="908" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hogarth-Harlot-1_crop.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail from William Hogarth\'s <a href="/wiki/A_Harlot%27s_Progress" title="A Harlot&#39;s Progress">A Harlot\'s Progress</a> (1732), showing Moll\'s arrival in London and her procurement by a pox-ridden madame.</div></div></div>\n<p>The presence of prostitution in London during the 17th and 18th centuries is demonstrated by the publication of directories. <i>The Wandering Whore</i> was published during the <a href="/wiki/Restoration_(England)" title="Restoration (England)">Restoration</a> period, and listed streets where prostitutes might be found and the locations of brothels.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> <i>A Catalogue of Jilts, Cracks &amp; Prostitutes</i> was published towards the end of the 17th century and catalogued the physical attributes of 21 women who could be found about <a href="/wiki/St_Bartholomew-the-Great" title="St Bartholomew-the-Great">St Bartholomew\'s Church</a> during <a href="/wiki/Bartholomew_Fair" title="Bartholomew Fair">Bartholomew Fair</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Smithfield,_London" title="Smithfield, London">Smithfield</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Harris%27s_List_of_Covent_Garden_Ladies" title="Harris&#39;s List of Covent Garden Ladies">Harris\'s List of Covent Garden Ladies</a></i> was published during the second half of the 18th century as a pocketbook. It described the physical appearance and sexual specialities of about 120\xe2\x80\x93190 prostitutes who worked in and around <a href="/wiki/Covent_Garden" title="Covent Garden">Covent Garden</a> (then a well-known <a href="/wiki/Red-light_district" title="Red-light district">red-light district</a>) along with their addresses and prices.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Bullough argues that prostitution in 18th-century Britain was a convenience to men of all social statuses, and an economic necessity for many poor women, and was tolerated by society. Nevertheless, a ban on brothel-keeping was included in the <a href="/wiki/Disorderly_Houses_Act_1751" title="Disorderly Houses Act 1751">Disorderly Houses Act 1751</a> as part of legislation against <a href="/wiki/Public_nuisance" title="Public nuisance">public nuisance</a>. Towards the end of the century, public opinion began to turn against the sex trade, with reformers petitioning the authorities to take action.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism#19th_century" title="Evangelicalism">evangelical movement of the 19th century</a> denounced prostitutes and their clients as sinners, and society for tolerating it.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Vagrancy_Act_1824" title="Vagrancy Act 1824">Vagrancy Act 1824</a> introduced the term "<a href="/wiki/Common_prostitute" title="Common prostitute">common prostitute</a>" into <a href="/wiki/English_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="English Law">English Law</a> and criminalised prostitutes with a punishment of up to one month <a href="/wiki/Hard_labour" class="mw-redirect" title="Hard labour">hard labour</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The act also made it a crime for a man to live on the earnings of a prostitute (often known as "living off immoral earnings").<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Victorian_morality" title="Victorian morality">Victorian morality</a> held that prostitution was a terrible evil, for <a href="/wiki/Women_in_the_Victorian_era" title="Women in the Victorian era">the young women</a>, for <a href="/wiki/Victorian_masculinity" title="Victorian masculinity">the men</a> and for <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">all of society</a>. One of the first pieces of legislation introduced during the Victorian period to restrict prostitution was the <a href="/wiki/Town_Police_Clauses_Act_1847" title="Town Police Clauses Act 1847">Town Police Clauses Act 1847</a>, which made it an offence for common prostitutes to assemble at any "place of public resort" such as a <a href="/wiki/English_coffeehouses_in_the_17th_and_18th_centuries" title="English coffeehouses in the 17th and 18th centuries">coffee shop</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For several reasons prostitution was predominantly a <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working-class</a> occupation. For many women, their journey into prostitution was one of circumstance. During the 19th century the public began to concern itself with particular social problems; conversely, a view of the ideal woman began to emerge such as "<a href="/wiki/The_Angel_in_the_House" title="The Angel in the House">The Angel in the House</a>". The rise of middle-class domestic morality and the separation of men\'s and women\'s activity into <a href="/wiki/Separate_spheres" title="Separate spheres">separate spheres</a> made it increasingly hard for women to obtain work, causing an increase in such areas as the needle-trade, shop girls, agricultural gangs, factory work, and domestic servants,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> all occupations with long hours and low pay. Low earnings, it is argued,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> meant that women had to resort to prostitution to be able to provide for themselves and their families, particularly in households where the main breadwinner was no longer around. A study from the late Victorian period showed that more than 90 per cent of prostitutes in <a href="/wiki/Millbank_prison" class="mw-redirect" title="Millbank prison">Millbank prison</a> were the daughters of "unskilled and semiskilled working men", more than 50 per cent of whom had been servants, the rest having worked in dead-end jobs such as <a href="/wiki/Washerwoman" title="Washerwoman">laundering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charwoman" title="Charwoman">charring</a> (cleaning houses) and <a href="/wiki/Costermonger" title="Costermonger">street selling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalkowitz198216_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalkowitz198216-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The level of prostitution was high in Victorian England, but the nature of the occupation makes it difficult to establish the exact number of prostitutes in operation. Judicial reports of the years 1857 to 1869 show that prostitutes were more common in commercial ports and pleasure resorts and less so in hardware towns, cotton and linen manufacturing centres and woollen and worsted centres.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalkowitz198222_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalkowitz198222-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Westminster_Review" class="mw-redirect" title="Westminster Review">Westminster Review</a></i> placed the figure between 50,000 and 368,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESigworthWyke198077_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESigworthWyke198077-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> This would make prostitution the fourth-largest female occupation. One difficulty in calculating numbers is that In the 19th century the word "prostitute" was also used to refer to women who were living with men outside marriage, women who had had illegitimate children, and women who perhaps had relations with men for pleasure rather than money.<sup id="cite_ref-BL_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BL-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The police estimates of known prostitutes offer an entirely different figure.\n</p><p>Police estimates of known prostitutes:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESigworthWyke198077_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESigworthWyke198077-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Date\n</th>\n<th>London\n</th>\n<th>England and Wales\n</th></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td>1839\n</td>\n<td>6,371\n</td>\n<td>\xe2\x80\x93\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td>1841\n</td>\n<td>9,404\n</td>\n<td>\xe2\x80\x93\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td>1856\n</td>\n<td>8,600\n</td>\n<td>\xe2\x80\x93\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td>1858\n</td>\n<td>7,194\n</td>\n<td>27,113\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td>1859\n</td>\n<td>6,649\n</td>\n<td>28,743\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td>1861\n</td>\n<td>7,124\n</td>\n<td>29,572\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td>1862\n</td>\n<td>5,795\n</td>\n<td>28,449\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td>1863\n</td>\n<td>5,581\n</td>\n<td>27,411\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td>1864\n</td>\n<td>5,689\n</td>\n<td>26,802\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td>1865\n</td>\n<td>5,911\n</td>\n<td>26,213\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td>1866\n</td>\n<td>5,544\n</td>\n<td>24,717\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td>1867\n</td>\n<td>5,628\n</td>\n<td>24,999\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td>1888\n</td>\n<td>5,678\n</td>\n<td>24,311\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>However, this table relates only to prostitutes known to the police. The unreliability of statistics during the 19th century makes it unclear if prostitution was increasing or decreasing during this period, but there is no doubt that Victorians during the 1840s and 1850s thought that prostitution and venereal disease (as <a href="/wiki/Sexually_transmitted_infections" class="mw-redirect" title="Sexually transmitted infections">sexually transmitted infections</a> were called then) were increasing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESigworthWyke198080_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESigworthWyke198080-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Actresses were associated with prostitution in the public mind, and a woman\xe2\x80\x99s lack of respectability was indicated by her presence in a place of public entertainment. A series of small books, The Swell\xe2\x80\x99s Night Guides, listed the advantages and drawbacks of various theatres for men seeking pleasure, and gave advice on how to approach actresses. It warned men not to offer them money directly, but to say they wanted to hire them for private theatricals.<sup id="cite_ref-BL_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BL-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Selina_Rushbrook_(n%C3%A9e_Selina_Ann_Jenkins),_1905.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ec/Selina_Rushbrook_%28n%C3%A9e_Selina_Ann_Jenkins%29%2C_1905.jpg/170px-Selina_Rushbrook_%28n%C3%A9e_Selina_Ann_Jenkins%29%2C_1905.jpg" width="170" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ec/Selina_Rushbrook_%28n%C3%A9e_Selina_Ann_Jenkins%29%2C_1905.jpg/255px-Selina_Rushbrook_%28n%C3%A9e_Selina_Ann_Jenkins%29%2C_1905.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ec/Selina_Rushbrook_%28n%C3%A9e_Selina_Ann_Jenkins%29%2C_1905.jpg/340px-Selina_Rushbrook_%28n%C3%A9e_Selina_Ann_Jenkins%29%2C_1905.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1706" data-file-height="2527" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Selina_Rushbrook_(n%C3%A9e_Selina_Ann_Jenkins),_1905.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Selina_Rushbrook" title="Selina Rushbrook">Selina Rushbrook</a> (1880\xe2\x80\x931907), a Swansea prostitute</div></div></div>\n<p>Some prostitutes worked in <a href="/wiki/Red-light_district" title="Red-light district">red-light districts</a>, others in their own neighbourhoods. London\'s dockyards had a large population of prostitutes, and Granby Street, beside <a href="/wiki/London_Waterloo_station" title="London Waterloo station">Waterloo Station</a>, was well known for its "half naked" women in the windows.<sup id="cite_ref-BL_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BL-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Prostitutes also found work within the armed forces, mainly due to servicemen\'s forced celibacy and the conditions of the barracks the men were forced to endure.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESigworthWyke198088_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESigworthWyke198088-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The barracks were overcrowded and had a lack of ventilation and defective sanitation. Very few servicemen were permitted to marry, and even those were not given an allowance to support their wives, which occasionally lured them to become prostitutes as well.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESigworthWyke198089_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESigworthWyke198089-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Regulating prostitution was the government\'s attempt to control the high level of venereal disease in its armed forces. By 1864, one out of three sick cases in the army was caused by venereal disease; admissions into hospitals for gonorrhoea and syphilis reached 290.7 per 1,000 of total troop strength.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalkowitz198249_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalkowitz198249-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Public attention was drawn to prostitution in London by <a href="/wiki/William_Acton_(doctor)" title="William Acton (doctor)">William Acton</a>\'s controversial 1857 book <i><a href="/wiki/Prostitution,_Considered_in_Its_Moral,_Social,_and_Sanitary_Aspects" title="Prostitution, Considered in Its Moral, Social, and Sanitary Aspects">Prostitution, Considered in Its Moral, Social, and Sanitary Aspects</a></i>. It raised concerns that the city was the centre of moral decay in Britain and was infested with diseased prostitutes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson199352_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson199352-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Acton denounced low wages for women as one of the reasons why they would turn to prostitution, in contrast to the dominant perception among members of the middle and upper classes that women decided to become prostitutes because of an innate lustfulness and sinful nature.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeabodyRichardson1892_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeabodyRichardson1892-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Contagious_Diseases_Acts" title="Contagious Diseases Acts">Contagious Diseases Acts</a> were introduced in the 1860s, adopting the French system of licensed prostitution, with the goal of minimising venereal disease. Prostitutes were subjected to compulsory checks for venereal disease, and imprisonment until cured. Young women officially became prostitutes and were trapped for life in the system. After a nationwide crusade led by <a href="/wiki/Josephine_Butler" title="Josephine Butler">Josephine Butler</a>, legalised prostitution was stopped in 1886 and Butler became a sort of saviour to the girls she helped free. The <a href="/wiki/Criminal_Law_Amendment_Act_1885" title="Criminal Law Amendment Act 1885">Criminal Law Amendment Act 1885</a> made numerous changes that affected prostitution, including criminalising the act of procuring girls for prostitution by administering drugs or intimidation or fraud, suppressing brothels and raising the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_consent" title="Age of consent">age of consent</a> for young women from 12 to 16.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> This last provision undercut the supply of young prostitutes who were in highest demand. The new moral code meant that respectable men dared not be caught.<sup id="cite_ref-Hal\xc3\xa9vy_498_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hal\xc3\xa9vy_498-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is also some evidence of homosexual male prostitution in the Victorian period. Since homosexuality was illegal at this time, most of the information that we have comes from court cases. A few dozen report the closures of gay brothels, or pubs, but the most popular locations were the parks and the streets, particularly those near barracks.<sup id="cite_ref-BL_48-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BL-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the second half of the 20th century several attempts were made to reduce prostitution. The <a href="/wiki/Sexual_Offences_Act_1956" title="Sexual Offences Act 1956">Sexual Offences Act 1956</a> included sections making <a href="/wiki/Brothel" title="Brothel">brothel</a>-keeping an offence. New restrictions to reduce street prostitution were added with the <a href="/wiki/Street_Offences_Act_1959" title="Street Offences Act 1959">Street Offences Act 1959</a>, which stated: "It shall be an offence for a common prostitute to loiter or <a href="/wiki/Solicitation" title="Solicitation">solicit</a> in a street or public place for the purpose of prostitution." As a result, many prostitutes left the street for fear of imprisonment. As Donald Thomas put it in <i>Villains\' Paradise</i>:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>The Street Offences Act of 1959 sought to prevent the public nuisance of having prostitutes on the pavements and thereby turned most of them into \'<a href="/wiki/Call_girl" title="Call girl">call-girls</a>\'. The mass availability of the telephone as much as moral determination by the authorities made the change possible. Fines of \xc2\xa360 for pavement soliciting and possible imprisonment under the new law accelerated it.<sup id="cite_ref-Villains_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Villains-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>The penalty for living off immoral earnings was also increased, to a maximum of seven years\' imprisonment.\n</p><p>The publication of directories of prostitutes (also known as <a href="/wiki/Contact_magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="Contact magazine">contact magazines</a>) was legally challenged in 1962 when Frederick Charles Shaw published the <i>Ladies Directory</i>, a guide to London prostitutes. He was convicted of "conspiracy to corrupt public morals" and appealed on the grounds that no such offence existed. The House of Lords dismissed the appeal, in effect creating a new common law offence.<sup id="cite_ref-Villains_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Villains-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shaw_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In a later piece of legislation, some of the activities carried out by prostitutes\' clients were criminalised. The <a href="/wiki/Sexual_Offences_Act_1985" title="Sexual Offences Act 1985">Sexual Offences Act 1985</a> created the two new offences of <a href="/wiki/Kerb_crawling" class="mw-redirect" title="Kerb crawling">kerb crawling</a> and persistently soliciting women for the purposes of prostitution.<sup id="cite_ref-legislation_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-legislation-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>An increase in the number of prostitutes originating from overseas in the 21st century led to concerns regarding allegations of <a href="/wiki/Human_trafficking" title="Human trafficking">human trafficking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forced_prostitution" title="Forced prostitution">forced prostitution</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Sexual_Offences_Act_2003" title="Sexual Offences Act 2003">Sexual Offences Act 2003</a> included sections making <a href="/wiki/Sex_trafficking" title="Sex trafficking">sex trafficking</a> a specific offence. A <a href="/wiki/Home_Office" title="Home Office">Home Office</a> review <i>Paying the Price</i> was carried out in 2004. It focused on projects to divert women from entering prostitution, and to engage with those already trapped to help them exit.<sup id="cite_ref-Munn_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Munn-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> A second Home Office review <i>Tackling the demand for prostitution</i> (2008) proposed the development of a new offence to criminalise those who pay for sex with a person who is being controlled against their wishes for someone else\'s gain.<sup id="cite_ref-Munn_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Munn-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> This approach to prostitution began to make legislative progress in 2008, as <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Jacqui_Smith" title="Jacqui Smith">Jacqui Smith</a> announced that paying for sex from a prostitute under the control of a pimp would become a criminal offence. Clients could also face rape charges for knowingly paying for sex from an illegally trafficked woman, and first-time offenders could face charges.<sup id="cite_ref-Smithlaw_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smithlaw-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Policing_and_Crime_Act_2009" title="Policing and Crime Act 2009">Policing and Crime Act 2009</a> made it an offence to pay for the services of a prostitute "subjected to force",<sup id="cite_ref-nodo50_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nodo50-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> introduced closure orders for brothels and made other provisions in relation to prostitution.\n</p><p>Some differing local approaches to policing have been tried. In <a href="/wiki/Ipswich" title="Ipswich">Ipswich</a> a version of the "Nordic model" was implemented in 2007 following the <a href="/wiki/Ipswich_serial_murders" title="Ipswich serial murders">Ipswich serial murders</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a> unsuccessful initiatives to suppress prostitution were followed in 2014 by the introduction of local regulation.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> An experimental "managed" prostitution zone was set up in <a href="/wiki/Holbeck" title="Holbeck">Holbeck</a>, Leeds to allow prostitutes to work in a designated area between 7&#160;pm and 7&#160;am without the risk of prosecution.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> It was made permanent in January 2016<sup id="cite_ref-Kirby_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirby-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> and the BBC made the documentary series <i><a href="/wiki/Sex,_Drugs_%26_Murder:_Life_in_the_Red_Light_Zone" title="Sex, Drugs &amp; Murder: Life in the Red Light Zone">Sex, Drugs &amp; Murder: Life in the Red Light Zone</a></i> about the zone.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gwent_Police" title="Gwent Police">Gwent Police</a> considered similar plans in 2015 for a part of <a href="/wiki/Pillgwenlly" title="Pillgwenlly">Pillgwenlly</a> in <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Wales" title="Newport, Wales">Newport</a>, Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_legal_status">Current legal status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Current legal status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="England_and_Wales">England and Wales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: England and Wales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Policing_and_Crime_Act_2009" title="Policing and Crime Act 2009">Policing and Crime Act 2009</a> (together with the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_Offences_Act_2003" title="Sexual Offences Act 2003">Sexual Offences Act 2003</a>) replaced most aspects of previous legislation relating to prostitution, although previous acts still remain in force. Working as a prostitute in private is not an offence, and neither is working as an outcall escort. Nor is it illegal for prostitutes to sell sex at a brothel provided they are not involved in management or control of the brothel.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Street_prostitution" title="Street prostitution">Street prostitution</a>, however, is illegal.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Street_prostitution">Street prostitution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Street prostitution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>It is an offence to loiter or <a href="/wiki/Solicitation" title="Solicitation">solicit</a> persistently in a street or public place for the purpose of offering one\'s services as a prostitute. The term "prostitute" is defined as someone who has offered or provided sexual services to another person in return for a financial arrangement on at least one previous occasion. The laws on soliciting and loitering for the purposes of prostitution were amended by the 2009 act. The main differences involve the shifting of focus from the prostitutes to the customers. Before 1 April 2010, it was illegal for a customer to kerb crawl/solicit only if this was done "persistently", or "in a manner likely to cause annoyance". Today, all forms of public solicitation by a customer are illegal, regardless of the manner in which the prostitute was solicited. The act also makes it an offence for someone to pay or promise to pay a prostitute who has been subject to "exploitive conduct". The law now applies to male as well as female prostitutes because the term "common prostitute" has been replaced with "person". Before 1 April 2010, a prostitute was committing a crime by soliciting/loitering in a public place more than once in a period of one month. Today, he/she commits a crime if he/she does it more than once in a period of three months. Sentencing options for loitering available to the courts include a fine of up to \xc2\xa31000, the issuing of a <a href="/wiki/Anti-social_behaviour_order" title="Anti-social behaviour order">Criminal behaviour order</a> and the requirement to attend rehabilitation meetings using an <a href="/wiki/Policing_and_Crime_Act_2009#Prostitution" title="Policing and Crime Act 2009">Engagement and Support Order</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Brothels">Brothels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Brothels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_Offences_Act_1956" title="Sexual Offences Act 1956">Sexual Offences Act 1956</a>, It is an offence for a person to keep a brothel, or to manage, or act or assist in the management of, a brothel.<sup id="cite_ref-sexualoffencesact1956_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sexualoffencesact1956-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Section 33a of the Sexual Offences Act 2003 also updated this law and made it an offence for a person to keep, or to manage, or act or assist in the\nmanagement of, a brothel to which people resort for practices involving\nprostitution (whether or not also for other practices).  This section provided a maximum penalty of seven years in prison and minimum of six months.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prostitutes_cautions">Prostitutes cautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Prostitutes cautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>To demonstrate "persistence" under the current legislation, two police officers must witness the activity and administer a non-statutory <i>prostitute caution</i>. This caution differs from an ordinary <a href="/wiki/Police_caution" title="Police caution">police caution</a> in that the behaviour leading to a caution need not itself be evidence of a criminal offence. There is no requirement for a man or woman to admit guilt before being given a <i>prostitutes caution</i> and there is no right of appeal.<sup id="cite_ref-CPS_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CPS-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Even if no criminal action is pursued, the caution remains on the individual\'s criminal record and may affect their future employment prospects.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Customers">Customers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Customers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Soliciting someone for the purpose of obtaining their sexual services as a prostitute is an offence if the soliciting takes place in a street or public place (whether in a vehicle or not). This is a broader restriction than the 1985 ban on kerb-crawling. It is now also an offence to make or promise payment for the sexual services of a prostitute if the prostitute has been subjected to "exploitative conduct" (force, threats or deception) to bring about such an arrangement for gain. This is a <a href="/wiki/Strict_liability" title="Strict liability">strict liability</a> offence (clients can be prosecuted even if they did not know the prostitute was forced).<sup id="cite_ref-nodo50_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nodo50-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Additionally there exists an offence of paying for sexual services of a child (anyone under 18).\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_parties">Third parties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Third parties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>There are various third party offences relating to prostitution. For instance, causing or inciting another person to become a prostitute for gain is an offence.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pimping" class="mw-redirect" title="Pimping">Pimping</a> (controlling the activities of another person relating to that person\'s prostitution for gain) is also illegal.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Similarly <a href="/wiki/Brothel" title="Brothel">brothelkeeping</a> is illegal. It is an offence for a person to keep, or to manage, or act or assist in the management of, a brothel.<sup id="cite_ref-legislation_b_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-legislation_b-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Note that the definition of a brothel in English law is "a place where people are allowed to resort for illicit intercourse". It is not necessary that the premises are used for the purposes of prostitution since a brothel exists wherever more than one person offers sexual intercourse, whether for payment or not. Thus the prohibition on brothels covers premises where people go for non-commercial sexual encounters, such as certain saunas and adult clubs.<sup id="cite_ref-CPS_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CPS-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> However, premises which are frequented by men for intercourse with only one woman are not a brothel,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and this is so whether she is a tenant or not.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Thus in practice to avoid committing this offence a prostitute who works in private must work alone. Additionally there exists an offence of causing, inciting, controlling, arranging or facilitating <a href="/wiki/Child_prostitution" title="Child prostitution">child prostitution</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Advertising">Advertising</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Advertising">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Advertising for the services of prostitutes has traditionally been expressed in euphemistic language, partly as an attempt to avoid prosecution and partly as an expression of British cultural values. Prostitutes have advertised in specialist <a href="/wiki/Contact_magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="Contact magazine">contact magazines</a> for decades despite a common law offence of "conspiracy to corrupt public morals" which was created in 1962 to prohibit such advertising.<sup id="cite_ref-Shaw_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Adverts for prostitutes have also been placed in public telephone boxes (where they are known as <a href="/wiki/Tart_card" title="Tart card">tart cards</a>) despite the <a href="/wiki/Criminal_Justice_and_Police_Act_2001" title="Criminal Justice and Police Act 2001">Criminal Justice and Police Act 2001</a> making such advertising an offence. Newspaper advertising has been used since advertising in newspapers is not in itself illegal. However, a newspaper which carries advertising for illegal establishments and activities such as brothels or venues where sexual services are offered illegally may be liable to prosecution for money laundering offences under the <a href="/wiki/Proceeds_of_Crime_Act_2002" title="Proceeds of Crime Act 2002">Proceeds of Crime Act 2002</a>. This is the case even if such places are advertised under the guise of massage parlours and saunas. Some police forces have local policies in place for enforcement against prostitution services advertised in the local press. The Newspaper Society\'s guidelines suggest that their members (the majority of local newspapers) refuse to carry advertisements for sexual services.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Newspaper companies nowadays often adopt a policy of refusing all advertisements for <i>personal services</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-CPS_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CPS-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Internet_prostitution" title="Internet prostitution">Internet advertising</a> is now widely used by prostitutes, primarily in the form of specialist websites.<sup id="cite_ref-Economist_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">Social media</a> have also become a common way to attract clients.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> An unsuccessful <a href="/wiki/Private_member%27s_bill" title="Private member&#39;s bill">private member\'s bill</a> to prohibit the advertising of prostitution, the Advertising of Prostitution (Prohibition) Bill 2015\xe2\x80\x9316, was introduced by <a href="/wiki/Lord_McColl_of_Dulwich" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord McColl of Dulwich">Lord McColl of Dulwich</a> in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> in June 2015<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> and backed by the Christian advocacy group <a href="/wiki/Christian_Action_Research_and_Education" title="Christian Action Research and Education">CARE</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Ireland">Northern Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Northern Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prostitution_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Prostitution in Northern Ireland">Prostitution in Northern Ireland</a></div>\n<p>It has been illegal to pay for sex in Northern Ireland since 1 June 2015 as a result of the Human Trafficking and Exploitation (Criminal Justice and Support for Victims) Act (Northern Ireland) 2015 which was enacted in January 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Previously, prostitution in Northern Ireland was governed by similar legal restraints to those in the rest of the United Kingdom.  The first prosecution for paying for the services of a prostitute was brought in October 2017 in <a href="/wiki/Dungannon" title="Dungannon">Dungannon</a>, Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scotland">Scotland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Scotland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prostitution_in_Scotland" title="Prostitution in Scotland">Prostitution in Scotland</a></div>\n<p>Since <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolution</a> in 1998 the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a> has started to pursue an independent policy to prostitution which had been historically similar to England since the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Act of Union</a>.\n</p><p>Street prostitution is dealt with under the <a href="/wiki/Civic_Government_(Scotland)_Act_1982" title="Civic Government (Scotland) Act 1982">Civic Government (Scotland) Act 1982</a>, section 46(1). <a href="/wiki/Kerb_crawler" title="Kerb crawler">Kerb crawling</a>, soliciting a prostitute for sex in a public place, and loitering for the same purpose are also criminal under the Prostitution (Public Places) (Scotland) Act 2007. There was formerly no specific offence directed at clients in Scotland in contrast to the "kerb crawling" offence in England and Wales in the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_Offences_Act_1985" title="Sexual Offences Act 1985">Sexual Offences Act 1985</a>.\n</p><p>A Prostitution Tolerance Zones Bill was introduced into the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a> but failed to become law. A number of attempts have been made to criminalise the purchase of sex but all have failed.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reform_of_prostitution_laws">Reform of prostitution laws</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Reform of prostitution laws">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>There is a debate about the possible reform of prostitution laws in the UK. It centres around the question of whether new legislation is necessary or desirable, and if so which of the three main options for change the UK should follow. Proponents of regulation argue for a system modelled on those used to regulate <a href="/wiki/Prostitution_in_Germany" title="Prostitution in Germany">prostitution in Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prostitution_in_the_Netherlands" title="Prostitution in the Netherlands">prostitution in the Netherlands</a>. Proponents of decriminalisation argue for an unregulated system similar to that covering <a href="/wiki/Prostitution_in_New_Zealand" title="Prostitution in New Zealand">prostitution in New Zealand</a> and parts of Australia. Proponents of sex buyer laws argue for a system in which it is illegal to pay for sex, as is the case with <a href="/wiki/Prostitution_in_Sweden" title="Prostitution in Sweden">prostitution in Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prostitution_in_Norway" title="Prostitution in Norway">prostitution in Norway</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prostitution_in_Iceland" title="Prostitution in Iceland">prostitution in Iceland</a>. This last option is sometimes described as the <i>Nordic model of prostitution</i>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_opinion">Public opinion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Public opinion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A CATI survey conducted in January 2008 revealed the following answers:\n</p><p><i>Paying for sex exploits women and should be a criminal offence</i>: 44% of the total respondents agreed (65% of those aged 18\xe2\x80\x9324 agree; 48% of all women agree, 39% of men agree)\n</p><p><i>Paying for sex exploits women but should not be a criminal offence</i>: 21% of the total respondents agreed\n</p><p><i>Paying for sex does not exploit women and should not be a criminal offence</i>: 17% of the total respondents agreed\n</p><p><i>Paying for sex does not exploit women but should be a criminal offence</i>: 8% of the total respondents agreed<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An Ipsos-Mori poll conducted in July and August 2008 showed that 61% of women and 42% of men thought that paying for sex was "unacceptable", while 65% of women and 40% of men said selling sex was "unacceptable". Young people were the most opposed to prostitution: 64% of the youth said that paying for sex was "unacceptable" and 69% believed that selling sex was "unacceptable"; older people had more relaxed attitudes about prostitution (men over 55 were the most accepting of buying sex).\nOf all the people who were questioned, 60% would feel ashamed if they found out a family member was working as a prostitute, while 43% thought it should be illegal to pay for sex; however, 58% supported making it illegal to pay for sex if "it will help reduce the numbers of women and children being trafficked into the UK for sexual exploitation".<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation">Regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 2006, the Labour government raised the possibility of loosening the prostitution laws and allowing small <a href="/wiki/Brothel" title="Brothel">brothels</a> in <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a>. According to the law that is still current, one prostitute may work from an indoor premises, but if there are two or more prostitutes the place is considered a brothel and it is an offence. Historically, local police forces have wavered between <a href="/wiki/Zero_tolerance" title="Zero tolerance">zero tolerance</a> of prostitution and unofficial <a href="/wiki/Red_light_districts" class="mw-redirect" title="Red light districts">red light districts</a>. Three British ministers, <a href="/wiki/Vernon_Coaker" title="Vernon Coaker">Vernon Coaker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Follett_(politician)" title="Barbara Follett (politician)">Barbara Follett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vera_Baird" title="Vera Baird">Vera Baird</a>, visited the Netherlands to study their approach to the sex trade, and came to the conclusion that their policy of legal prostitution was not effective, and therefore ruled out the legalisation of prostitution in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Plans to allow "mini brothels" were abandoned, after fears that such establishments would bring pimps and drug dealers into residential areas.\n</p><p>On the subject of local regulation, a spokeswoman for the English Collective of Prostitutes commented in 2016: "A managed zone is no substitute for decriminalisation. Some women complain that the police wash their hands of the area and they feel segregated from the protection of the local community."<sup id="cite_ref-Kirby_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirby-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decriminalisation">Decriminalisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Decriminalisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Like many other countries, the UK has <a href="/wiki/Sex_workers%27_rights" title="Sex workers&#39; rights">sex workers\' rights</a> groups, which argue that the best solution for the problems associated with prostitution is decriminalisation. These groups have criticised the provisions from the <a href="/wiki/Policing_and_Crime_Act_2009" title="Policing and Crime Act 2009">Policing and Crime Act 2009</a>. The <a href="/wiki/English_Collective_of_Prostitutes" title="English Collective of Prostitutes">English Collective of Prostitutes</a> (ECP), founded in 1975, campaigns for the decriminalisation of prostitution, sex workers\' right to recognition and safety, and financial alternatives so that no one is forced into prostitution by poverty; in addition the ECP provides information, help and support to individual prostitutes and others concerned with sex workers\' rights. One member, Nikki Adams, said that the government was overstating the extent of the trafficking problem, and that most prostitution was <a href="/wiki/Consensual_sex" class="mw-redirect" title="Consensual sex">consensual</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smithlaw_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smithlaw-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The UK based <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Sex_Workers" title="International Union of Sex Workers">International Union of Sex Workers</a> (IUSW), part of <a href="/wiki/GMB_(trade_union)" title="GMB (trade union)">GMB Trade Union</a>, campaigns for the <a href="/wiki/Labour_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour rights">labour rights</a> of those who work in the sex industry.\n</p><p>In 2010, in response to the <a href="/wiki/Bradford_murders" title="Bradford murders">Bradford murders</a> of three prostitutes, the new Conservative prime minister <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> said that the decriminalisation of prostitution should be "looked at again". He also called for tougher action on <a href="/wiki/Kerb-crawling" class="mw-redirect" title="Kerb-crawling">kerb-crawling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drug_abuse" class="mw-redirect" title="Drug abuse">drug abuse</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Chief_Police_Officers" title="Association of Chief Police Officers">Association of Chief Police Officers</a> suggested that designated red-light zones and decriminalised brothels might help to improve prostitutes\' safety.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Defendants in a test case in Manchester attempted to use the <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Act_1998" title="Human Rights Act 1998">Human Rights Act 1998</a> to argue that the law against brothelkeeping breached their human rights by not allowing them to work together as prostitutes in safety. However, the case collapsed in 2016 without a verdict.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian29March2016_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian29March2016-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 2016, <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Corbyn" title="Jeremy Corbyn">Jeremy Corbyn</a>, speaking to students at <a href="/wiki/Goldsmiths,_University_of_London" title="Goldsmiths, University of London">Goldsmith\'s University</a>, said that he was "in favour of decriminalising the sex industry".<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In May 2016 the <a href="/wiki/Home_Affairs_Select_Committee" title="Home Affairs Select Committee">Home Affairs Select Committee</a>, headed by <a href="/wiki/Keith_Vaz" title="Keith Vaz">Keith Vaz</a>, investigated prostitution laws in Britain. The committee called on <a href="/wiki/Brooke_Magnanti" title="Brooke Magnanti">Brooke Magnanti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paris_Lees" title="Paris Lees">Paris Lees</a> to give evidence about sex work conditions in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The pair suggested<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> that the past criminal records<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> of those arrested for prostitution-related crimes should be eliminated.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The committee\'s interim report was published in July 2016. It recommended that soliciting should be decriminalised and that sex workers should be allowed to share premises, while laws allowing the prosecution of those who use brothels to control or exploit sex workers should be retained.<sup id="cite_ref-HOSC_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOSC-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> It also recommended that past criminal records for prostitution should be removed,<sup id="cite_ref-Evans_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> as suggested by Maganti and Lees. Sex worker nonprofits called the apparent U-turn decision "a stunning victory for sex workers and our demands for decriminalisation" and "a giant step forward for sex workers\' rights in the UK."<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="The_.22Nordic_model.22_of_prostitution"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_&quot;Nordic_model&quot;_of_prostitution">The "Nordic model" of prostitution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: The &quot;Nordic model&quot; of prostitution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The focus of those who oppose the legalisation of prostitution is the ethical argument that prostitution is inherently exploitative, a view held by many in the Government and the police.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Additionally it is argued that the legalisation of prostitution would result in an increase in human trafficking and crime.
