An example offered by anti-prostitution activists is that of <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a> in the Netherlands, which experienced severe problems with human trafficking and crime in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> At the time the mayor of Amsterdam, <a href="/wiki/Job_Cohen" title="Job Cohen">Job Cohen</a>, said about legal prostitution in his city: "We\'ve realised this is no longer about small-scale entrepreneurs, but that big crime organisations are involved here in trafficking women, drugs, killings and other criminal activities"  and "We realise that this [legal prostitution] hasn\'t worked, that trafficking in women continues. Women are now moved around more, making police work more difficult."<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2007 <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_House_of_Commons" title="Leader of the House of Commons">Commons Leader</a> <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Harman" title="Harriet Harman">Harriet Harman</a> proposed that the "demand side" of prostitution should be tackled by making it illegal to pay for sex.<sup id="cite_ref-Smithlaw_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smithlaw-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Ministers pointed to <a href="/wiki/Prostitution_in_Sweden" title="Prostitution in Sweden">Sweden</a>, where purchasing sexual services is a criminal offence.\n</p><p>In March 2014 an all-party parliamentary group in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> issued a report called <i>Shifting the Burden</i><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> which claimed that the current legislation is complicated and confusing. The report expressed concern at the difficulty of successfully prosecuting the sexual abuse of girls and the rape of trafficked women. The report proposed the introduction of the <i>Nordic model of prostitution</i> to England and Wales,<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> consolidating current legislation into a single act with a general offence for the purchase of sexual services. It also suggested re-examining the definition of force and coercion in the Policing and Crime Act 2009 and raising the age at which strict liability is established under the Sexual Offences Act 2003 from 13 to 16.<sup id="cite_ref-Munn_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Munn-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 2014 <a href="/wiki/Fiona_Mactaggart" title="Fiona Mactaggart">Fiona Mactaggart</a> MP added an amendment to the <a href="/wiki/Modern_Slavery_Act_2015" title="Modern Slavery Act 2015">Modern Slavery Bill</a>, a bill consolidating and simplifying slavery and trafficking offences into one law.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Mactaggart\'s amendment aimed to criminalise the purchase of sex ("procuring sex for payment").<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> In response <a href="/wiki/Shadow_Home_Secretary" title="Shadow Home Secretary">Shadow Home Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Yvette_Cooper" title="Yvette Cooper">Yvette Cooper</a> put forward an alternative amendment which called for a period of review and research.<sup id="cite_ref-Corvid_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Corvid-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Mactaggart\'s amendment was subsequently dropped before the bill became law in March 2015 despite its initially having received cross-party support.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 2016 the <a href="/wiki/Home_Affairs_Select_Committee" title="Home Affairs Select Committee">Home Affairs Select Committee</a> began an inquiry into prostitution legislation, including trying to assess "whether the balance in the burden of criminality should shift to those who pay for sex rather than those who sell it".<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> On the subject of the "sex buyer law" (as it termed the Nordic model), the committee\'s interim report said:<sup id="cite_ref-HOSC_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOSC-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The sex buyer law... is based on the premise that prostitution is morally wrong and should therefore be illegal, whereas at present the law makes no such moral judgement... the sex buyer law makes no attempt to discriminate between prostitution which occurs between two consenting adults, and that which involves exploitation. Much of the rhetoric also denies sex workers the opportunity to speak for themselves and to make their own choices... We are not yet convinced that the sex buyer law would be effective in reducing demand or in improving the lives of sex workers...\n</p></blockquote>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Crimes_against_prostitutes">Crimes against prostitutes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Crimes against prostitutes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Violence_against_prostitutes" title="Violence against prostitutes">Violence against prostitutes</a></div>\n<p>Prostitutes are routinely victims of crime as a result of the social and legal status of their profession. 180 sex workers were murdered in Britain between 1990 and 2015, according to figures given by the <a href="/wiki/UK_Network_of_Sex_Work_Projects#National_Ugly_Mugs_(NUM)" title="UK Network of Sex Work Projects">National Ugly Mugs (NUM)</a> scheme.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Of the last 11 to die, nine were migrants.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> University of Leeds research in 2015 found that 47% of prostitutes had been victims of crime, including rape and robbery, while 36% had received threatening <a href="/wiki/Text_messaging" title="Text messaging">texts</a>, telephone calls or emails.<sup id="cite_ref-DianeTaylor_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DianeTaylor-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The mortality rate for sex workers is 12 times higher than the national average.<sup id="cite_ref-pop_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pop-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> There have been a number of websites which have allowed prostitutes to publish warnings regarding potentially dangerous clients. In 2007 the <i>Saafe</i> forum (Support and Advice for Escorts) created a centralised function using <a href="/wiki/RSS" title="RSS">RSS</a> from existing sites. This did not work as well as envisaged and was ended in 2010. In 2011 the <a href="/wiki/Home_Office" title="Home Office">Home Office</a> announced a pilot scheme for a national online network (<i>National Ugly Mugs</i>) to collate and distribute information.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The scheme was launched in 2012 and run by the <a href="/wiki/UK_Network_of_Sex_Work_Projects" title="UK Network of Sex Work Projects">UK Network of Sex Work Projects</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> It has continued after its 12-month pilot period and is still in operation.<sup id="cite_ref-Economist_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Serial_murders">Serial murders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Serial murders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>There have been a number of notable serial murders of prostitutes in the United Kingdom.\n</p>\n<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Whitechapel_murders" title="Whitechapel murders">Whitechapel murders</a> were a series of eleven unsolved murders of women committed in or near the impoverished <a href="/wiki/Whitechapel_District_(Metropolis)" title="Whitechapel District (Metropolis)">Whitechapel district</a> in the <a href="/wiki/East_End_of_London" title="East End of London">East End of London</a> between 3 April 1888 and 13 February 1891. Most, if not all, of the victims were prostitutes. Some of the attacks were notable on account of post-mortem abdominal mutilations. Some or all of them have variously been ascribed to the unidentified serial killer known as <a href="/wiki/Jack_the_Ripper" title="Jack the Ripper">Jack the Ripper</a>.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Jack_the_Stripper" class="mw-redirect" title="Jack the Stripper">Jack the Stripper</a> murders (also known as the "<a href="/wiki/Hammersmith" title="Hammersmith">Hammersmith</a> murders", "Hammersmith nudes" or "nude murders") were a series of between six and eight unsolved murders of prostitutes that took place in London between 1964 and 1965. All the victims were found dead in and around the <a href="/wiki/River_Thames" title="River Thames">River Thames</a>, all had been strangled and all were naked. "Jack the Stripper" was the nickname given to the unknown serial killer.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>In 1981 <a href="/wiki/Peter_Sutcliffe" title="Peter Sutcliffe">Peter Sutcliffe</a> (popularly referred to as the "Yorkshire Ripper") was convicted of a series of murders of thirteen women including a number of prostitutes that took place between 1975 and 1980 in and around <a href="/wiki/West_Yorkshire" title="West Yorkshire">West Yorkshire</a>. Sutcliffe was sentenced to life imprisonment.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Ipswich_serial_murders" title="Ipswich serial murders">Ipswich serial murders</a> took place between 30 October and 10 December 2006, when the bodies of five murdered women were discovered at different locations near <a href="/wiki/Ipswich" title="Ipswich">Ipswich</a>, Suffolk. All the victims were prostitutes from the Ipswich area. <a href="/wiki/Steve_Wright_(serial_killer)" title="Steve Wright (serial killer)">Steve Wright</a> was sentenced to <a href="/wiki/Life_imprisonment" title="Life imprisonment">life imprisonment</a> \xe2\x80\x93 with recommendation of a <a href="/wiki/Whole_life_tariff" class="mw-redirect" title="Whole life tariff">whole life tariff</a> \xe2\x80\x93 for the murders. The case received high media attention.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Bradford_murders" title="Bradford murders">Bradford murders</a> took place in 2009\xe2\x80\x9310 in <a href="/wiki/Bradford" title="Bradford">Bradford</a>. Three prostitutes were killed. On 24 May 2010 Stephen Shaun Griffiths was arrested and subsequently charged with the crime.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Griffiths was convicted of all three murders on 21 December 2010 after pleading guilty. He was given a life sentence.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sex_trafficking">Sex trafficking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Sex trafficking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Human_trafficking_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Human trafficking in the United Kingdom">Human trafficking in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>In the early 2000s there was growing concern about <a href="/wiki/Human_trafficking" title="Human trafficking">human trafficking</a>, in particular allegations regarding the trafficking of women and underage girls into the UK for forced prostitution. As a result, the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_Offences_Act_2003" title="Sexual Offences Act 2003">Sexual Offences Act 2003</a> included sections dealing with cases of sex trafficking. Section 57 of the Act covers trafficking <i>into</i> the UK for sexual exploitation. Offences relating to trafficking <i>within</i> and <i>out of</i> the UK are contained in sections 58 and 59. These offences apply in England and Wales and Northern Ireland, with section 22 of the Criminal Justice (Scotland) Act 2003 providing similar offences for Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The act uses a much looser definition of "trafficking" than the international definition used in the <a href="/wiki/Protocol_to_Prevent,_Suppress_and_Punish_Trafficking_in_Persons,_especially_Women_and_Children" class="mw-redirect" title="Protocol to Prevent, Suppress and Punish Trafficking in Persons, especially Women and Children">UN Protocol</a>, lacking any requirement that a person is trafficked for sex against their will or with the use of coercion or force. Simply arranging or facilitating the arrival in the United Kingdom of another person for the purpose of prostitution is considered trafficking. Hence the act covers the movement of all sex workers, including willing professionals who are simply travelling in search of a better income.<sup id="cite_ref-legislation_b_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-legislation_b-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2005 a high-profile court case resulted in the conviction of five Albanians who trafficked a 16-year-old Lithuanian girl and forced her to have sex with as many as 10 men a day.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> A 2007 UN report identified the major sources of trafficked persons include Thailand, China, Nigeria, Albania, Bulgaria, Belarus, Moldova and Ukraine.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The British government signed the <a href="/wiki/Trafficking_in_human_beings#Council_of_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Trafficking in human beings">Council of Europe Convention on Action against Trafficking in Human Beings</a> in March 2007, and ratified it in December 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In July 2008 <i>Operation Pentameter Two</i>, the UK\'s biggest ever investigation into sex trafficking, announced 528 arrests but resulted in no convictions.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> A study carried out in 2011 by <a href="/wiki/London_Metropolitan_University" title="London Metropolitan University">London Metropolitan University</a> and funded by the Government\'s <a href="/wiki/Economic_and_Social_Research_Council" title="Economic and Social Research Council">Economic and Social Research Council</a> found that 6% of prostitutes "felt" they were "deceived and forced" into the work. Commenting on the low figure, Dr Nick Mai said that "the large majority of migrant workers in the UK sex industry are not forced or trafficked" and that "working in the sex industry is often a way for migrants to avoid the unrewarding and sometimes exploitative conditions they meet in non-sexual jobs."<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> However, the ESRC survey remains controversial as its data are derived from post-facto interviews with sex workers whose susceptibility to <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_Syndrome" class="mw-redirect" title="Stockholm Syndrome">Stockholm Syndrome</a> and other psychological traumas are well-documented.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">United States Department of State</a> <a href="/wiki/Office_to_Monitor_and_Combat_Trafficking_in_Persons" title="Office to Monitor and Combat Trafficking in Persons">Office to Monitor and Combat Trafficking in Persons</a> ranks the UK as a \'<a href="/wiki/Trafficking_in_Persons_Report#Ranking_System" title="Trafficking in Persons Report">Tier 1</a>\' country.<sup id="cite_ref-state18_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state18-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_figures_in_UK_prostitution">Notable figures in UK prostitution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Notable figures in UK prostitution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/John_Rykener" class="mw-redirect" title="John Rykener">John Rykener</a> was a 14th-century transvestite male prostitute who worked mainly in London.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Margaret_Fernseed" title="Margaret Fernseed">Margaret Fernseed</a> was an English prostitute, brothel keeper and murderer during the late 16th and early 17th centuries.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Damaris_Page" title="Damaris Page">Damaris Page</a> was a London brothel keeper, entrepreneur and property developer, and one of the most successful and famous prostitutes of the 17th century.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Cresswell" title="Elizabeth Cresswell">Elizabeth Cresswell</a> was one of the most successful prostitutes and brothel keepers of the English 17th century.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Sally_Lodge" title="Sally Lodge">Sally Lodge</a> was an English prostitute and brothel keeper in late-17th- and early-18th-century London.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Sally_Salisbury" title="Sally Salisbury">Sally Salisbury</a> was a celebrated prostitute in early 18th-century London who was the lover of many notable members of society, and socialised with many others.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Constantia_Jones" title="Constantia Jones">Constantia Jones</a> was a prostitute in 18th-century London who was sentenced to hang for stealing from one of her clients.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Betty_Careless" title="Betty Careless">Betty Careless</a> was a notorious prostitute and later <a href="/wiki/Bagnio" title="Bagnio">bagnio</a>-owner in 18th-century London.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Dora_Noyce" title="Dora Noyce">Dora Noyce</a>, Edinburgh brothel-keeper in the mid-20th century.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Cynthia_Payne" title="Cynthia Payne">Cynthia Payne</a>, brothel keeper in  Streatham, London</li></ul>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Vicky_de_Lambray" title="Vicky de Lambray">Vicky de Lambray</a> was a 20th-century British transvestite male prostitute who became a favourite of <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Street" title="Fleet Street">Fleet Street</a> gossip columnists.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lindi_St_Clair" title="Lindi St Clair">Lindi St Clair</a> is a 20\xe2\x80\x9321st-century English author, political campaigner for prostitutes\' rights and former prostitute.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Sheila_Vogel-Coupe" title="Sheila Vogel-Coupe">Sheila Vogel-Coupe</a> is a British prostitute who was reported in 2014 to be the oldest working prostitute in the United Kingdom at the age of 85.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">\n<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="flag" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/32px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" width="32" height="16" class="noviewer thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/48px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 1.5x,
